<result>No other layer backstops this. Confirmed: the middleware is the sole enforcement point, and a malformed `Content-Length` silently defaults to 0 (allowed through) rather than being rejected conservatively.

### Spec Compliance — ✅ [src/fold_at_scripps/middleware.py:9-25] / ⚠️ [src/fold_at_scripps/middleware.py:19-22]
- `BodySizeLimitMiddleware(BaseHTTPMiddleware)` with `__init__(app, max_bytes)` — ✅ matches brief exactly.
- `dispatch` reads `content-length`, returns 413 `JSONResponse({"detail": ...})` before calling `call_next` when over limit — ✅ (`middleware.py:22`, return precedes `call_next` at line 23; body is never awaited on the reject path).
- Registered in `main.py:46` via `app.add_middleware(BodySizeLimitMiddleware, max_bytes=settings.max_upload_bytes)` — ✅ exact match, `settings.max_upload_bytes` exists (`config.py:24`, `int`, default 100 MB).
- Malformed content-length doesn't crash — ✅ (`ValueError` caught), but ⚠️ it silently coerces to `declared = 0`, i.e., a garbage header **always passes through** with no size check at all, rather than being rejected or handled conservatively. This matches the brief's verbatim code, so it's spec-compliant by the letter of the brief, but it's a real gap the brief itself baked in.
- Tests: over-limit → 413 with truthy detail (`test_body_limit.py:19-22`); under-limit → 404 passthrough (`test_body_limit.py:25-28`) — ✅ genuine, uses real ASGI app via `ASGITransport`, ordinary settings/env-var flow, no mocking of the code under test.
- Chunked-body limitation noted, not silently swept under the rug — ✅ documented in report "Concerns" section, matches brief scope (brief's Step 2 code has no stream-consumption logic).

### Strengths
- Faithful, minimal implementation of the brief's verbatim code — no scope creep.
- Registration order argument (before `SessionMiddleware`) is correct and consistent with the "doesn't touch session" reasoning in both brief and report.
- Tests exercise a real app via `ASGITransport`/`AsyncClient`, not unit-testing the middleware in isolation with mocks — good signal of genuineness.
- Report is honest about a real limitation (chunked/streaming bodies, malformed header → 0) instead of overstating coverage.
- `ruff format`/`ruff check` clean per report; both deviations documented (unused `pytest` import removed, one-line `JSONResponse` call) — cosmetic, correctly called out.

### Issues (Critical / Important / Minor)

**Important** — `src/fold_at_scripps/middleware.py:76-78` (brief) / `middleware.py:19-21` (impl): a malformed `Content-Length` (e.g. `"abc"`, negative, or a value with trailing garbage) is coerced to `declared = 0`, which means the check is skipped entirely — an attacker can bypass the entire cap by sending a non-numeric or malformed `Content-Length` header alongside an arbitrarily large body, since `BaseHTTPMiddleware`'s `call_next` will still buffer/forward the full body downstream. This satisfies the letter of "doesn't crash" but defeats the purpose of the cap for a trivially-forgeable header. A more defensive choice would be to reject (409/413) or treat a malformed header as "unknown → cap by streaming/inspecting actual bytes," or at minimum clamp to `max_bytes + 1` rather than `0`. This is inherited verbatim from the brief, so it's not an implementer deviation, but it's worth flagging since it is a genuine security-relevant gap in the delivered behavior, not merely cosmetic.

**Minor** — `src/fold_at_scripps/middleware.py:15` (impl): `dispatch`'s `call_next` parameter has no type annotation (brief's own snippet also omits it) — inconsistent with the "type hints on all function signatures" global convention. `starlette`'s `RequestResponseEndpoint` type is available for this. Low impact since `BaseHTTPMiddleware.dispatch`'s override signature is otherwise conventional and ruff doesn't flag it, but strictly it's a gap against the stated type-hint standard.

**Minor** — no test exercises the malformed/absent-edge-case behavior (e.g., `Content-Length: not-a-number` or negative value) even though the report explicitly calls out that behavior as a known "concern." Given it's called out as a concern, a regression test pinning the current (permissive) behavior would have been low-cost and valuable, though not required by the brief's Step 1 test list.

### Assessment — **Task quality:** Approved
**Reasoning:** The implementation matches the brief's interfaces and required behavior precisely: correct middleware signature, correct pre-body-read short-circuit, correct registration call, and genuine passing tests for both directions. The one substantive concern (malformed `Content-Length` silently bypassing the cap) is inherited verbatim from the brief's own reference implementation and is explicitly disclosed in the report rather than hidden — this is a spec/design gap to raise with whoever owns Plan 10, not an implementation defect introduced by this task. No critical issues (doesn't block correctly, doesn't crash, is registered), so this task is approved as delivered; the malformed-header behavior should be tracked as a follow-up hardening item rather than grounds for rework here.</result>

<result>No `Any` type usage (only the English word "any" in a comment, which is fine). Production `get_engine()` uses the default `QueuePool` (not NullPool) — `acquire_scheduler_lock` calls `engine.connect()` which checks out a connection from that pool. Since it's held open for the process lifetime and never returned to the pool until shutdown, this is correct and matches the report's stated reasoning. No conflict with `pool_pre_ping`.

Everything checks out. Here is the review.

### Spec Compliance — ✅

- `locking.py:12` `acquire_scheduler_lock(engine: AsyncEngine) -&gt; AsyncConnection | None` — ✅ dedicated connection via `engine.connect()`, `SELECT pg_try_advisory_lock(:key)` with fixed key `_SCHEDULER_LOCK_KEY = 0x666F6C6473636864`, returns open connection on success, closes + returns `None` on contention, closes + re-raises on exception (`locking.py:20-30`).
- `main.py:52-61` `run_scheduler` acquires lock via `acquire_scheduler_lock(get_engine())` before `fail_orphaned_runs`/`run_forever`; `None` → `logger.error` + `raise SystemExit(1)` (`main.py:53-55`); success path holds `lock_conn` and only closes it in `finally` after `run_forever()` returns/raises — i.e., for the process lifetime, since `run_forever` (`service.py:81-94`) loops until `CancelledError`. ✅
- `configure_logging` called in both `run_scheduler` (`main.py:51`) and `main()` (`main.py:66`) — idempotent per `logging_config.py` (`dictConfig` full replace each call), consistent with brief's intent (brief only showed it in `main`, but calling it twice is harmless and the report explicitly flags this deviation with sound reasoning). ⚠️ (deviation from literal snippet, but explicitly disclosed, non-breaking, and arguably an improvement).
- `test_locking.py` — `@pytest.mark.integration` (registered in `pyproject.toml:71`), `poolclass=NullPool` (`test_locking.py:23`), sequence first→conn, second→None, close first, third→conn, cleanup via `engine.dispose()` in `finally`. ✅ Matches brief exactly.

### Strengths

- Correctly reasons about session-scoped advisory locks vs. connection pooling — the `NullPool` justification in the test comment is accurate and well-explained (a pooled `close()` returns the connection without ending the Postgres session, which would leave the lock held).
- Exception path in `acquire_scheduler_lock` closes the connection before re-raising, preventing a leaked connection on query failure.
- `run_scheduler`'s `finally: await lock_conn.close()` correctly releases the lock on any exit path (including unexpected exceptions bubbling out of `run_forever`, which only happens on `CancelledError` per `service.py:90-91`), so no lock leakage across process restarts.
- Module logger added consistently with existing codebase conventions (matches pattern in `service.py`).
- Report is honest and specific — flags the `configure_logging` placement deviation and its rationale rather than silently diverging from the brief.

### Issues

None Critical or Important. Two Minor/cosmetic observations:

**Minor** — `main.py:51` and `main.py:66`: `configure_logging` is called twice on the `main()` → `run_scheduler()` path (once in `main`, once at the top of `run_scheduler`). It's idempotent (full `dictConfig` replace) so functionally harmless, but it does mean `run_scheduler` has a side effect (global logging config mutation) that's redundant when invoked via the console-script entry point, and slightly surprising for anyone calling `run_scheduler()` directly in a context where they've already configured logging differently — it will silently stomp their config. Not a functional bug for this codebase's only two call sites, just a minor coupling smell. Fix (optional): call `configure_logging` only in `main()`, per the brief's original snippet, or accept the duplication as intentional defense with a one-line comment noting why it's duplicated.

**Minor** — `locking.py:8`: the comment "Fixed 64-bit key (`fold-sched`)" documents provenance but there's no runtime assertion/test that the key actually fits `pg_advisory_lock`'s signed 64-bit bigint domain. `0x666F6C6473636864` = 7,382,924,006,344,566,884, which is within `int64` range (&lt; 2^63-1 ≈ 9.22e18), so it's fine as-is — flagging only because a future edit to this "any constant works" key could silently overflow into `OverflowError`/driver-level rejection with no guardrail. Not worth blocking on.

### Assessment

**Task quality:** Approved

**Reasoning:** All spec requirements are met precisely: the lock helper's signature, query, and success/failure/exception semantics match the brief; `run_scheduler` acquires the lock before any scheduling work and fails fast with `SystemExit(1)` on contention; the held connection is correctly kept open (not returned to the pool) for the process lifetime, which is the crux of the single-scheduler guarantee; the test uses `NullPool` and validates the full acquire/contend/release/reacquire cycle exactly as specified. No `Any` typing, stable fixed lock key, clean exception handling with no connection leaks. The only deviation from the literal brief (double `configure_logging` call) is disclosed, idempotent, and non-breaking — not a spec or quality defect.</result>

<result>Confirmed: the lock connection's liveness is never re-checked, and `run_forever` swallows exceptions and continues. If Postgres restarts (the compose service is `restart: unless-stopped`), the held advisory-lock connection drops → the lock releases on the server side → this scheduler keeps polling (creating fresh sessions from the pool, which reconnect fine) while no longer holding the lock. A second `fold-scheduler` (or a systemd restart of the same unit that races) could then acquire the lock, defeating the single-scheduler guarantee. This is the classic lease-vs-lock weakness. It's an edge case (requires Postgres restart while scheduler runs), and single-node deployment with one scheduler unit makes concurrent acquisition unlikely — but the docs explicitly promote this lock as the safety mechanism.

I now have a complete, traced picture. Let me write the verdict.

---

**Verdict:** Ship with nits

The deployment artifacts, backend, and frontend cohere into a deployable system, and the accumulated deferrals (body-limit, advisory lock, code-split, logging, secret guard, SPA serving) are all resolved correctly. No blocking defects — nothing would hard-fail a careful deploy that follows the docs, and the two operational rough edges below self-recover. The most impactful item is the systemd PATH gap, which I'm flagging as a strong nit because it *will* surprise a first-time deployer.

### Blocking (must fix before merge)
None. I traced a fresh deploy end-to-end and found no step that unconditionally fails, no wrong path in the shipped env/units, no missing migration wiring, and no secret leak.

### Non-blocking (should / nice) — ranked, most severe first

1. **CONFIRMED — systemd PATH will not include `uv` or `autobio` for a typical install** (`deploy/fold-api.service:12-13`, `deploy/fold-scheduler.service:12`, `docs/DEPLOYMENT.md:30-31,21-22`). systemd services run with a minimal `PATH` (`/usr/local/sbin:/usr/local/bin:/usr/sbin:/usr/bin`), *not* the `fold` user's login-shell PATH. The standard `uv` install puts the binary in `~/.local/bin/uv` (= `/home/fold/.local/bin` given the `--create-home` user), which is not on that PATH, so `/usr/bin/env uv run …` fails with "uv: No such file or directory". Same for the scheduler's `subprocess.run(["autobio", …])` (`autobio_executor.py:24,31,50`), which resolves via the process PATH. The docs say "on the PATH of the fold user," conflating login-shell PATH with systemd's. Fix: either add `Environment=PATH=/home/fold/.local/bin:/usr/local/bin:/usr/bin:/bin` (and the docker/nvidia bin dirs) to both units, or use absolute paths (`ExecStart=/home/fold/.local/bin/uv run …`), or install `uv`/`autobio` into `/usr/local/bin`. This is the one item most likely to make a real first deploy fail.

2. **CONFIRMED — cold-boot race with the Postgres container (self-healing, noisy)** (`fold-api.service:4`, `fold-scheduler.service:4`, `docs/DEPLOYMENT.md`). `After=docker.service` orders the units after the Docker *daemon*, not after Postgres is accepting connections. On a host reboot, the API's `ExecStartPre` alembic migration and the scheduler's `acquire_scheduler_lock`/`fail_orphaned_runs` run before the DB is ready, crash, and rely on `Restart=on-failure` (5s) to eventually succeed. It recovers, but produces failed-start noise on every boot. Nice-to-have: a short `ExecStartPre` that waits for `pg_isready`, or a documented note that initial restarts on boot are expected.

3. **PLAUSIBLE — the advisory lock is a session-lifetime lease, not re-validated** (`scheduler/main.py:52-61`, `scheduler/service.py:81-94`). The lock is held on one dedicated connection for the process lifetime, but `run_forever` swallows all exceptions and never checks that connection's liveness. Because `docker-compose.yml` sets Postgres `restart: unless-stopped`, a Postgres restart drops the lock-holding connection (releasing the server-side advisory lock) while this scheduler keeps polling via freshly-reconnected pool sessions. A second scheduler could then acquire the lock, defeating the single-scheduler guarantee the docs advertise (`DEPLOYMENT.md:300-304`). Edge case on a single-node/single-unit deploy, but worth a comment or a periodic `SELECT 1` on `lock_conn` that exits on failure so systemd cleanly restarts and re-acquires.

4. **Triage of the logged Minors — all correctly deferrable:**
   - `fold.env.example` omits `FOLD_SCHEDULER_POLL_INTERVAL` — safe (2.0s default in `config.py:22`); the commented-line follow-up is fine. Defer.
   - `main._DEV_SECRET_KEY` duplicates `config.py`'s default literal — test-covered by `test_startup_guard.py`; harmless duplication. Defer.
   - Double `configure_logging` (scheduler `main()` at :66 then `run_scheduler()` at :51; app also idempotent) — `dictConfig` is idempotent; redundant but not a bug. Defer.
   - Chunked/no-Content-Length bodies uncapped — accepted scope; uvicorn backstops. Defer.
   - Code-split regex redundancy (`react-router` covered by `react-router-dom` prefix match) — cosmetic. Defer.

5. **PLAUSIBLE, very minor — SPA serves stale/500 if `index.html` is deleted post-boot** (`main.py:64-76`). `_mount_spa` guards on `dist.is_dir()` at startup but not that `index.html` exists; if the dir exists without an index, the catch-all `FileResponse(index_file)` raises at request time. Only reachable via a broken `build-frontend`; not worth blocking.

### Verified strengths

- **Deployment coherence (traced end-to-end):** `make build-frontend` (Dockerfile `dist` scratch stage → `--output type=local`) writes `frontend/dist`, which `FOLD_FRONTEND_DIST=/opt/fold-at-scripps/frontend/dist` (absolute in `fold.env.example`) points the API at — the relative `frontend_dist` default is only a dev fallback, so the CWD-resolution concern is moot in production. `npm ci` has its `package-lock.json`. Migrations run *only* on the API unit's `ExecStartPre`; `env.py` drives the async engine from `FOLD_DATABASE_URL` via `get_settings()`, so the systemd migration step is correctly wired. The scheduler unit correctly omits migrations. `After=fold-api.service` on the scheduler is *correct* ordering: `Type=simple` + `ExecStartPre` means the API is not "active" until migrations finish, so the scheduler starts against a migrated DB.
- **Secret guard × suite:** `conftest.py` `os.environ.setdefault("FOLD_SECRET_KEY", …)` at import time lets the module-level `app = create_app()` collect without raising; individual tests `monkeypatch.setenv` + `get_settings.cache_clear()` to exercise both guard branches. The `fold.env.example` placeholder (`CHANGE-ME-to-a-long-random-secret`) genuinely differs from the sentinel (`dev-insecure-secret-change-me`), so an operator who copies the example and forgets to edit it still boots (with a placeholder secret) — the guard only blocks the *literal dev default*. Verified: a real deploy that leaves `FOLD_DEBUG` unset (default `False`) and the *actual* dev default would refuse to boot. No real secret in any tracked file.
- **Deferrals resolved:** body-limit rejects malformed/negative Content-Length with 400 (no silent bypass) and 413 over-limit, both before routing/DB; middleware ordering is benign (Session outermost, BodySizeLimit inner — 413 still precedes routing). Advisory lock is held-for-lifetime with fail-fast `SystemExit(1)`. Code-split preserves the route tree byte-for-byte with a working Suspense fallback (`Loading.tsx` exists, `createQueryClient` exists, `renderWithProviders` still used by other tests). SPA catch-all is traversal-guarded (`dist_root in candidate.parents`) and the documented 404→405 for unmatched non-GET is expected and explicitly tested (`test_spa.py`).</result>
